There is no word in English of what Claus was trying to say.  In Spanish it is called enchilado.  It means you are suffering the effects of something that is too spicy and you are in an ecstatic state of euphoria from the pain of eating too much hot stuff.
"It's very spicy."  "I love the flavor."
I started making my own hot sauce.   Easy 3 ingredients.  1.  The peppers of choice.  2.  Sliced onions,  4 onions to 1 pepper. About two fistful of salt.  This makes about half gallon of delicious hot sauce.  The salt draws out the onion juice and you just let it sit in a dark place for a few days.  You can add vinegar or saurkraut brine if you need that sour flavor but it is perfect with just onions and salt.

Respectfully, I don't think Ed understands logarithmic scales.  The Scoville scale is, in fact, linear and the perception of hotness is logarithmic.  So Pepper X won't taste 3x as hot as the Carolina Reaper.  It will taste hotter, but not as hot as the Scoville rating difference would suggest.

This whole capcaisin culture is fascinating to me. In terms of ethnopharmacology (to give it a fancy word), the hallmarks of the pain trance that the Scovilles cause all technically could be called psychedelic. The "(head) high" Sean often references, along with all the varied effects, sometimes even including mild hallucinations and almost always involving the creatively expressed emotional honesty that comes with shared bonding in anguish, seems like a truly transformative experience. i never understand exactly what is meant by "the scale is ligarithmic". the ammount of SHU in a pepper is literally defined by the capsaÃ¯cinoid content of a product in ppm multiplied by 16 (at least for the 2 main capsaÃ¯cinoids naturally present in peppers, other naturally occuring capsaÃ¯cinoids are not as "spicy"). hence, pure capsaÃ¯cine scores 16.000.000 SHU (16 times 1.000.000 (parts per million)). this means a carolina reaper has 100.000 parts capsaÃ¯cine for every 1.000.000 parts of pepper, and pepper x has 162.000 parts of capsaÃ¯cine for every 1.000.000 parts of pepper. The scoville scale was designed in 1912 i believe, and the original scale was based on how many dilutions where requered before a chili extract was no longer experienced as "spicy" by taste testers. now adays we measure SHU in an objective way, by determining capsaÃ¯cine content. the conversion factor of 16 between ppm and SHU was implemented to make sure that the objectively measured results more or less correspond with the subjective research done back in 1912. the fact that dilutions where used to define SHU in 1912 does not mean that a pepper with twice the amount of SHU containts 10X the ammount of capsaÃ¯cine. however the eater experiences it is subjective, but the correlation between SHU and capsaÃ¯cinoid content is definitly linear, not logarithmic.

I remember when I was in college we had a seminar on what makes food spicy. A food chemist described how originally the Scoville test involved someone tasting a pepper and then it was based on the amount of sugar water needed to make that taste dissipate. Crazy how much science in the realm of food chemistry has advanced. I don't even think it would have been humanly possible to verify a Scoville level above the 1 millions back then until the more advanced system was created. Why build for heat first and flavor second? Flavor is everything. Number one. I live with two Jamaican sisters who can raise a few blisters. Every savory dish they make will contain the scotch bonnets and variants we grow. The purple bonnets that I brought from Jamaica are smoky and hot. We have a variety, some from Grace Co., sweet reds from a family grower in Portland Parish, gorgeous red hats. This obsession with heat first and flavor secondarily seems more of a dude challenge with alpha peppers than a culinary experience. If we can only put a tiny amount into a dish and lose the flavor, it seems a tail wagging a hot dog. Is this what men can produce? A bro hug is cool, but what is the cost? The cost is flavor. 2.6 million Scoville is only a bar to be beaten. 350 to 500 Scovillle  with amazing flavor is a dish to be eaten.

I met Klaus once in Copenhagen at an event he was doing, he was coming out with these ghost chili sausages and I got to try one, and I saw Klaus was there and I wanted to say hi, and honestly, he was one of the nicest people I have ever met. I feel like at the time, he was kinda big here in Denmark, and I was worried when I met him he was just doing it for his 15 minutes, but boy was I wrong. I like spicy food myself, and I did a chili tasting like he does with a friend and I told Klaus about it, and he told me about some of his stories also. While we were chatting a crew from a TV station came up to him and asked him for an interview, he told them. "Not now, can't you see I am talking with my chili colleague here". And then we chatted a bit more, and he asked me about my opinion of the sausage, and then asked if I wanted one more with him, so we ate one, took a picture together after. I can really say he does it because he has huge passion for it, and he seems to really love to engage with his fans, the fame seems secondary to him. I do hope one day I cross path with him again :) Super wholesome guy.

So the system is based on the "power" of the fruit in a general sense,  and the total SHU drops as the pepper is more diluted in other stuff, so a fresh pepper is inherently less SHU than a dried one simply due to the water content in it.    But however it works it is not a realm I ever wish to delve,  jalapeno peppers tend to be too much for me ğŸ¤£ğŸ¤£  and those are way down that list of hot things near the bottom.
